Chapter on Calculation of Inheritance. Two in the base of the problem [ 3264 (12) If one of the two numbers is a part of the other, the larger suffices for the smaller. After division, one wife got... 2÷2=1 6÷2=3 Method of division: In this problem, two wives got one portion which cannot be divided by two. Similarly, two brothers got three portions which cannot be divided by two brothers. But both have twos, so they multiplied the base of four by one number, i.e., two, and it became eight. The calculator calculation will be: Deceased 100, 2 wives, 2 brothers, 25, 75, each gets 12.5, each gets 37.5. Since the calculator decimal is unknown, the two wives got 25, so they halved it; each wife got 12.5. Similarly, the two brothers got 75, so they halved it; each brother got 37.5. This is an example of tamāthal. 3264 (12) If one of the two numbers is a part of the other, the larger number will suffice for the smaller, like four wives and two brothers. When you multiply by four, it will suffice instead of two. This is an example of tadākhul. In this problem, four is the larger number and two is the smaller number. Two is contained in four twice. Therefore, multiplying the base problem by the larger number is sufficient for the smaller number. This will give the correction. The problem will be formed as: In 4, correction 16. 4 wives: 4x4=16. 2 brothers: 1, 4, 3, 12. Each gets 1. Each gets 6. After correction, the two brothers got 3x4=12. After correction, the four wives got 1x4=4. The method of correction.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamīrī covers two fundamental methods of correcting inheritance problems: tamāthal (equality) and tadākhul (interlocking). The author first presents a tamāthal example: two wives and two brothers. The two wives receive 1/4 of the estate (25 out of 100), while the two brothers receive the remaining 3/4 (75) as residuaries. Since the shares cannot be divided evenly among the heirs of each category (1 share among 2 wives, 3 shares among 2 brothers), the base number (4) is multiplied by 2 (the number common to both categories) to get 8. This makes the shares divisible: each wife gets 12.5 and each brother gets 37.5. The author then introduces the tadākhul rule: if one number is a part of another (e.g., 2 is part of 4), it suffices to multiply by the larger number. The example given is 4 wives and 2 brothers. The base is 4 (since no children, wives get 1/4, brothers get 3/4). The shares: wives get 1 unit, brothers get 3 units. But 1 share cannot be divided among 4 wives, and 3 shares cannot be divided among 2 brothers. However, 2 (the number of brothers) is a part of 4 (the number of wives), so we multiply the base by 4, resulting in 16. The wives then get 4 shares (each 1), and the brothers get 12 shares (each 6). This demonstrates the tadākhul principle: the larger number suffices for the smaller.

Key Terms
تماثل (tamāthal): Equality between the number of heads in two categories of heirs; e.g., both categories have 2 heads.
تداخل (tadākhul): One number is a part of another; e.g., 2 is part of 4.
تصحيح (taṣḥīḥ): Correction of the base number to make shares divisible among heirs of the same category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a problem with 4 wives and 2 brothers, what is the multiplier for taṣḥīḥ?
A) 2
B) 4
C) 8
D) 6

Key Takeaway
Tamāthal and tadākhul are two of the four relationships that determine the multiplier in taṣḥīḥ, saving calculation time.

Tawāfuq: Applying the Rule of Agreement in Inheritance Correction
Explains tawāfuq rule for correcting inheritance bases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify tawāfuq, compute the wafq, and derive the corrected inheritance base in Hanafi farā'iḍ.

Core Idea
Tawāfuq: when heirlist numbers share a common factor, use the wafq of one times the other to correct the base.

In Hanafi inheritance, when fractional shares cannot be divided evenly among heirs, the base problem must be corrected. Tawāfuq is the third method—used when the numbers of two heir groups have a common factor greater than one. This lesson walks you through the rule with the example of four wives, one sister, and six paternal uncles, showing how to find the wafq and multiplier to achieve whole-number shares.

Guided Path
1
List heirs and their shares
Identify all heirs and their prescribed shares: 4 wives (1/4), 1 sister (1/2), 6 paternal uncles (asabah, remainder = 1/4). No hajb applies.
The base problem uses denominators: 1/4, 1/2, and asabah takes the rest.
2
Set the initial base problem
The base number (asl al-mas'alah) is the LCM of the denominators: 4 (since 1/4 and 1/2 have denominator 4, and asabah needs no denominator). So base = 4. Wives get 1 share, sister gets 2 shares, uncles get 1 share.
Base = 4: wives 1, sister 2, uncles 1. Total shares = 4.
3
Check divisibility per heir count
Wives' 1 share must be divided among 4 wives → not divisible (fraction). Uncles' 1 share among 6 uncles → not divisible. Sister's 2 shares for 1 person is fine.
Two categories are problematic: 4 wives and 6 uncles.
4
Examine the relationship between the two numbers
Compare 4 and 6. They share a common factor 2 (both even). This is tawāfuq bi-n-nisf (agreement by half).
Tawāfuq means there is a common divisor >1. The wafq is the quotient after dividing by that common factor.
5
Compute the wafq and multiplier
Take the wafq of one number: half of 4 = 2, or half of 6 = 3. Multiply the wafq of one by the whole other: 2 × 6 = 12, or 4 × 3 = 12. Either gives 12.
The multiplier (12) is the product that will be used to correct the base.
6
Apply the multiplier to the base
Multiply the base (4) by the multiplier (12): 4 × 12 = 48. The corrected base becomes 48.
Now all shares become whole numbers: wives' new share = 1×12 = 12 (each gets 3), sister = 2×12 = 24, uncles = 1×12 = 12 (each gets 2).
7
Verify the distribution
Total shares in corrected base: 12+24+12 = 48, matching the base. Each heir's share is now divisible by their count.
Tawāfuq ensures every heir gets a whole number of parts without fractions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

3265 (13) If one of the two numbers agrees with the other, multiply the wafq of one by the whole of the other, then (multiply) the product by the base of the problem. For example, four women and a sister and six paternal uncles: six agrees with four by half, so multiply half of one by the whole of the other, then (multiply) the product by the base of the problem; it becomes forty-eight, and from it the correction is made. After division, each brother got... After division, each wife got... 12÷2=6, 4÷4=1. Method of division: In this case, there are no children, so the four wives were given a quarter of the entire wealth, and the remaining three-quarters were given to the two brothers as residuaries. The problem was made from four. In this problem, four was the larger number and two the smaller. Two is interlocked with four. So the base of four was multiplied by four, making sixteen, and correction was made from sixteen. The calculator calculation will be: 100, 4 wives, 2 brothers, 25, 75, each gets 6.25, each gets 37.5. The four wives got shares, divided among four; each wife got 6.25. The two brothers got 75, divided by 2; each brother got 37.5. This is an example of tadākhul. [3265 (13) If there is agreement (tawāfuq) between the numbers of two parties, then multiply the wafq of one by the whole of the other. Then multiply the product by the base of the problem. For instance, four wives, one sister, and six uncles. Six and four have tawāfuq bi-n-nisf (commensurability by half), so multiply half of one by the whole of the other. Then multiply by the base of the problem; it becomes forty-eight, and from it the problem corrects. When the problem is corrected, multiply each heir's shares by the estate, then divide the product by the number from which the problem was made. Each heir's right will appear. This is an example of tawāfuq. Because four wives and six uncles have agreement; the number two divides both. So tawāfuq bi-n-nisf occurred. Therefore, first multiply six by two instead of four, getting twelve. Then multiply twelve by the base of four, getting forty-eight. And from this, correction is made. The problem will be formed as:
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage introduces tawāfuq (commensurability), the third method of correcting inheritance problems. The author first recaps the tadākhul example (4 wives, 2 brothers) from the previous page, showing the calculator method. Then he presents the new rule: when the numbers of two categories of heirs have a common factor (tawāfuq), multiply the wafk (the factor-reduced number) of one by the full number of the other. The example: 4 wives, 1 sister, and 6 uncles. The wives are 4, the uncles are 6. They share a common factor of 2, so the relationship is tawāfuq bi-n-nisf. The base problem is 4 (since wives get 1/4, sister gets 1/2, and uncles take the residue as asabah). To correct, first find the wafq of one: take half of one number (e.g., half of 4 is 2, or half of 6 is 3). Multiply that wafq by the whole of the other: e.g., 2 × 6 = 12 (or 4 × 3 = 12). Then multiply the product (12) by the base (4) to get 48. The correction is from 48. The explanation emphasizes that this ensures all shares become whole numbers divisible by the number of heads. This is a key technique in Hanafi inheritance law (farā'iḍ) for achieving equitable distribution without fractions.

Key Terms
توافق (tawāfuq): Agreement between two numbers in having a common factor greater than 1.
وفق (wafq): The quotient when a number is divided by its common factor with another number.
عصبة (asabah): Residuary heir who takes the remainder after fixed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a problem with 4 wives, 1 sister, and 6 uncles, what is the final corrected base number?
A) 12
B) 24
C) 48
D) 96

Key Takeaway
Tawāfuq corrects inheritance bases by using the wafq (common factor quotient) to find the smallest multiplier that makes all shares whole integers.

Tatakhul in Faraid: Four Wives and Two Brothers
Faraid calculation with tatakhul (interlocking).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify tatakhul between two heir group numbers, apply the correct multiplier for tashih, and distribute the estate accurately.

Core Idea
When one group's heir-count divides another's, multiply the base by the larger number — that's tatakhul.

This passage walks through a classic inheritance problem: a deceased leaves four wives and two brothers. The calculation illustrates the Hanafi rule of tatakhul, where one number is a factor of another, simplifying the correction step. Understanding this will let you handle similar cases efficiently.

Guided Path
1
Identify Heirs and Fixed Shares
List all heirs. Here: 4 wives (Quranic sharers, 1/4) and 2 brothers (asabah, residue). No children, so wives get 1/4, brothers get 3/4.
Wives' share is 1/4 because the deceased has no children.
2
Set the Base Number (Asl al-Mas'alah)
The base is the denominator of the fixed share(s). Here, 1/4 gives base 4. Initial shares: wives 1 part, brothers 3 parts.
Base is the LCD of the fixed shares. Only one fixed share, so base = 4.
3
Check for Tatakhul Between Group Numbers
Count individuals in each group: 4 wives and 2 brothers. Check if one number divides the other. 2 divides 4 → tatakhul exists.
Tatakhul means the smaller number is a divisor of the larger.
4
Apply Tashih Multiplier
When tatakhul is present, the multiplier is the larger number (4). Multiply the original base (4) by 4 → new base = 16. Multiply the initial shares: wives 1×4=4, brothers 3×4=12.
The multiplier is always the larger number when one divides the other.
5
Distribute to Each Heir
Divide each group's share by its number of individuals. Wives: 4 shares ÷ 4 = 1 each. Brothers: 12 shares ÷ 2 = 6 each. For an estate of 100 units, wives get 25 each, brothers 37.5 each.
The tashih ensures all shares are whole integers before dividing.

Commentary
This passage presents a complete faraid case with tatakhul. The author first states a rule in Arabic that is partially garbled but relates to handling cases where the number of heirs in one group divides into another. The Urdu explanation clarifies with an example: a deceased leaves 4 wives and 2 brothers, with no children. The wives receive their Quranic fixed share of 1/4, and the brothers receive the remaining 3/4 as residuaries (asabah). The base number (asl al-mas'alah) is set at 4, the denominator of the wives' share. Initially, the wives' share is one part out of four, and the brothers' share is three parts. However, these parts must be divided among the individual heirs: 4 wives need to share 1 part, and 2 brothers need to share 3 parts. To make the distribution integer, the base must be corrected (tashih). Since the number of brothers (2) divides the number of wives (4) — a relationship called tatakhul (interlocking) — the correct multiplier is the larger number, i.e., 4. Multiplying the original base of 4 by 4 gives a new base of 16. The shares are then multiplied by the same factor: the wives receive 1×4 = 4 parts, each wife getting 4/4 = 1 part; the brothers receive 3×4 = 12 parts, each brother getting 12/2 = 6 parts. The Urdu then shows a calculator approach using a total estate of 100 units: wives get 25 each, brothers get 37.5 each. This illustrates the principle of tatakhul where one number is a factor of the other, simplifying the correction.

Key Terms
تداخل (tatakhul): A relationship between two numbers where one number divides the other completely (interlocking/overlap).
تصحیح (tashih): The process of adjusting the base number of the inheritance problem to ensure that shares can be distributed as whole integers among the individual heirs.
اصل المسئلة (asl al-mas'alah): The base number of the inheritance problem, derived from the denominators of the fixed shares, used as the starting point for distribution.

Tawafuq in Faraid: The Case of Four Wives, One Sister, and Six Uncles
Faraid calculation with tawafuq between two groups of heirs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the correction multiplier when two groups of heirs have numbers with tawafuq (common factor) and apply it to distribute the inheritance correctly.

Core Idea
When two heir-group numbers share a common factor, the correction multiplier is the matching part of one times the whole of the other.

In this lesson, we work through a concrete faraid example where the numbers of wives and uncles are 4 and 6, which share a common factor of 2 (tawafuq bi al-nisf). You will learn how to determine the correction multiplier by taking half of one number and multiplying by the whole of the other, then adjusting the base and shares accordingly. This case demonstrates a critical step in tashih (correction) that prevents fractional shares.

Guided Path
1
Identify heirs and fixed shares
List all heirs and assign their Quranic shares: wives 1/4, sister 1/2, paternal uncles asabah (residue 1/4).
The shares must sum to 1 (1/4 + 1/2 + 1/4 = 1).
2
Set the base number
Find the least common multiple of the share denominators (4 and 2) → base 4. Assign initial shares: wives 1, sister 2, uncles 1.
Base 4 gives whole numbers for the fixed shares.
3
Check the relationship between the numbers of individuals in each group
Look at the actual numbers of recipients: 4 wives and 6 uncles. Determine if they have tawafuq (common factor >1).
Here, 4 and 6 share a common factor of 2, so tawafuq bi al-nisf.
4
Calculate the correction multiplier
Take the matching part (half of one number) and multiply by the whole of the other. For example, half of 4 is 2, multiply by 6 gives 12. Alternatively, half of 6 is 3, multiply by 4 also gives 12.
The correction multiplier is always the same regardless of which number you halve.
5
Apply the multiplier to correct the base and distribute
Multiply the original base (4) by the multiplier (12) to get corrected base 48. Multiply each heir's shares by 12, then divide by their group size to get individual shares.
Final shares: each wife gets 3, sister gets 24, each uncle gets 2.

Commentary
This passage presents a complete faraid case with tawafuq (agreement/common factor) between the numbers of two groups of heirs. The author first provides the Arabic rule: when two numbers have a common factor (tawafuq), you multiply the matching part (the number after dividing by the common factor) of one by the whole of the other, and then multiply the result into the base of the problem. The Urdu explanation clarifies with an example: a deceased leaves 4 wives, 1 sister, and 6 paternal uncles. The fixed shares: wives get 1/4 (fard), sister gets 1/2 (fard) because there are no children or siblings? Actually, in Hanafi faraid, a sister gets 1/2 when there are no children or father, but in this case, there may be no such impediment? The text shows sister getting 2 out of base? Wait, the base is 4 and sisters gets 2? That means sister's share is 2/4 = 1/2. Yes. Uncles get the residue as asabah: after wives (1/4) and sister (1/2), remainder is 1/4, which is assigned to uncles. The base number is 4. The shares: wives: 1 part, sister: 2 parts, uncles: 1 part. To distribute these among the individuals: 4 wives share 1 part, 1 sister gets 2 parts, 6 uncles share 1 part. The numbers of wives (4) and uncles (6) share a common factor of 2 (tawafuq bi al-nisf). To correct the base, we multiply the matching part of one (half of 4 = 2) by the whole of the other (6) to get 12 (or half of 6 = 3 times 4 = 12). Then multiply the original base (4) by 12 to get 48. The shares are multiplied accordingly: wives: 1×12=12 (each wife gets 12/4=3), sister: 2×12=24, uncles: 1×12=12 (each uncle gets 12/6=2). The author also shows a calculator method with estate 100: wives get 25 total (6.25 each), sister gets 50, uncles get 25 total (4.16 each). The general rule at the bottom of page 475 states: after correcting the base, multiply each heir's shares (from the corrected base) by the total estate, then divide by the corrected base to get each heir's portion. This passage beautifully demonstrates tawafuq and the practical steps for distribution.

Key Terms
توافق (tawafuq): A relationship between two numbers where they share a common factor greater than 1 (agreement).
بالعصف (bi al-nisf): By half; here it means the common factor is 2, so the numbers agree by half.
عصبہ (asabah): Residuary heir who takes the remaining inheritance after fixed shares; here paternal uncles are asabah bi-nafsihi.

Key Takeaway
When two groups of heirs have numbers with tawafuq, the correction multiplier is the matching part (number divided by common factor) of one multiplied by the whole of the other, not their product.

Tashih and Distribution in Faraid: A Worked Example
Worked example of tashih and share distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform tashih and calculate the final shares for each heir in a faraid case involving multiple heirs sharing a single fractional share.

Core Idea
When a single share cannot be divided equally among multiple heirs, tashih corrects the base number to give integer shares.

This lesson walks through a classic Hanafi inheritance problem: a deceased leaves four wives, one full sister, and six paternal uncles. The base number (asl) is 4, but the uncles' share of 1 portion must be divided among 6 uncles, which is not an integer. We apply tashih using the method of wafq (compatibility) to reach a new base of 48, then distribute the shares to each individual heir. The lesson also shows an alternative decimal method using a calculator.

Guided Path
1
Identify heirs and their prescribed shares
List all heirs and their Quranic or residuary shares. In this case: wives 1/4, sister 1/2, uncles as residue (1/4).
The shares add up to 1 (1/4+1/2+1/4).
2
Determine the base number (asl al-mas'alah)
Find the LCM of the denominators of the fractional shares. Denominators 4 and 2 give LCM=4. So asl=4. Wives get 1 share, sister 2 shares, uncles 1 share.
Asl=4.
3
Check for need of tashih
For each category with multiple individuals, check if the share portion is divisible by the number of individuals. Wives: 1 share among 4 wives → 1/4 not integer (need 0.25). But wait: wives get 1 share total, each gets 1/4 of the estate, not 1/4 of the share. Actually, the share portion for wives is 1 in asl=4, representing 1/4 of estate. For 4 wives, 1/4 of estate must be divided among them, so each gets (1/4)/4 = 1/16, which is fractional. So tashih is needed for wives too! However, in the example, the author only applies tashih for uncles because the wives' share (1) is a single share that can be represented as 1/4 of estate, and dividing 1 share among 4 is not integer, but typically tashih is applied when the number of heirs in a category is more than the share portion? Let me double-check the source: The author says 'In this case, six and four had compatibility...' referring to uncles. The wives had 1 share and 4 wives, but the author did not mention tashih for wives because 1/4 of the estate split among 4 wives gives 1/16 each, which is a fraction, but in the asl representation, the wives' share is 1 share out of 4 asl. To get integer shares for each wife, we would need to multiply the asl by the number of wives (4) to get 16, then each wife gets 1 share. But the author did not do that because the uncles' tashih already enlarged the base to 48, and that also made wives' shares integer: 12 shares among 4 wives = 3 each, which is integer. So the tashih for uncles indirectly resolved wives' fractional issue as well. So in this case, only one tashih was needed because the multiplier (12) made all shares divisible. So step: check each category; if any category has indivisibility, tashih is needed for that category, and the multiplier may resolve other categories too. In this example, uncles' share (1) not divisible by 6, so tashih needed. The wives' share (1) not divisible by 4, but since the same multiplier fixed it, no separate tashih required.
Here, only uncles trigger tashih because 1/6 is not integer. Wives also have 1/4 but are resolved by the same multiplier.
4
Perform tashih: find correction multiplier and new base
For the category needing tashih (uncles: n=6, share portion=1), find wafq (common factor) between n and asl (4). Common factor = 2. The multiplier = n * (asl / wafq) = 6 * (4/2) = 12. New base = asl * multiplier = 4 * 12 = 48.
Alternatively, multiplier = n / wafq * asl? The formula used by the author is clear: multiply n by (asl / wafq).
5
Distribute shares to individual heirs
After tashih, the share for each category is multiplied by the multiplier. Wives' share: 1 * 12 = 12 (for all 4 wives). Sister: 2 * 12 = 24. Uncles: 1 * 12 = 12. Then divide by number of individuals: each wife gets 12/4=3, sister gets 24, each uncle gets 12/6=2, out of total 48.
The result is integer shares. Also shown calculator method with estate=100 giving fractional outcomes.

In the mentioned example, the original case four was multiplied by twelve, giving a correction of forty-eight. So multiply the wives' share of one by twelve, getting twelve. This became the share of the four wives. Now divide it by four, each wife gets three. This extracted each wife's share. Or for example, the six uncles' share was one. Multiply twelve by one to get twelve. This became the share of the six uncles. Now divide twelve by six, each uncle gets two. This is each uncle's share out of forty-eight. See the detail in the method of correction. In the calculator calculation, there is no need to do this length; divide with fractions and decimal points.

Commentary
This passage is a faraid (inheritance calculation) lesson explaining how to distribute shares after performing tashih (correction of the base number). The author presents a worked example: a deceased leaves four wives, one full sister, and six paternal uncles. The original base number (asl al-mas'alah) is 4: the wives get 1/4 (1 share), the sister gets 1/2 (2 shares), and the uncles as asaba (residuary heirs) get the remaining 1/4 (1 share). Since the uncles' share of 1 cannot be divided among 6 uncles, tashih is required. Noticing compatibility (wafq) between 6 and 4 (common factor 2), the author multiplies 6 by (4/2)=12 to get a new base number of 48. After tashih, the shares become 12 for all wives (each gets 3), 24 for the sister, and 12 for all uncles (each gets 2). The author also shows a calculator method using decimal division from an estate of 100 rupees, giving fractional shares per heir. The commentary emphasizes the principle: after tashih, multiply each heir's asl share by the correction factor to find the actual portion. This is a standard Hanafi method to avoid fractional shares in inheritance distribution.

Key Terms
تصحیح (tashih): Correction of the base number (asl al-mas'alah) by multiplying it by a factor to make all heirs' shares whole numbers.
عاصبہ (asabah): Residuary heir who takes the remainder after fixed shares; here, paternal uncles.
فریضہ (faridah): The inheritance calculation or case; the base number of the problem.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tashih ensures each heir receives an integer number of shares by correcting the base number using a multiplier found through wafq (compatibility) between the number of heirs and the asl.

Munasakha: When an Heir Dies Before Distribution
General rule for inheritance when an heir dies before division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for munasakha to determine whether a new calculation is needed when an heir dies before estate distribution.

Core Idea
If the second deceased's share divides evenly among his heirs using the first tashih, no new calculation is needed.

Inheritance calculations can become complicated when one of the original heirs dies before the estate is distributed. The Hanafi school addresses this with the principle of munasakha (recurrence). This lesson explains the first step: checking whether the share received by the second deceased can be directly passed to his heirs using the same base number, or whether a combined calculation is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two deaths
Recognize that the first deceased's estate has not yet been distributed, and a second death has occurred among the heirs.
2
Determine the second deceased's share
Calculate what the second deceased would have received from the first estate using the original calculation (including tashih).
This share is expressed as a number of shares (e.g., 5 units after tashih).
3
Check divisibility among the second deceased's heirs
Examine whether this share can be divided evenly among the second deceased's heirs without needing a new tashih. If the number of heirs divides the share exactly, no new calculation is needed.
If not, a combined calculation (using the lcm of denominators from both estates) is required.
4
Apply the rule
If the share is divisible, distribute it directly. Otherwise, perform a new munasakha calculation combining both estates.
The text provides an example where the father's share of 5 units can be divided among 5 heirs, so the same tashih suffices.

Key Terms
مناسخہ (munasakha): A situation in inheritance where one or more heirs die before the estate of the first deceased is distributed, requiring a combined calculation.

Munasakha: Recalculating Shares When an Heir Dies
Rule for munasakha with two sub-cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method for munasakha by distinguishing between the two cases of divisibility and non-divisibility of the deceased heir's share.

Core Idea
If the first heir's share divides evenly among his heirs, no new base; otherwise, combine the two faraid problems.

Munasakha arises when an heir dies before the original estate is distributed. The Hanafi school provides a clear method: first check if the share of the deceased heir from the original estate can be divided evenly among his own heirs. If it can, the same base number works for both distributions. If not, a separate faraid calculation for the second death is needed, and then the two denominators must be combined by multiplication or reduced by common factors (tawafuq). This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine that the estate of the first deceased has not been distributed, and one of the heirs has died.
2
Check divisibility
Take the share that this heir was entitled to from the first estate. Divide that share by the number of his own heirs. If the division yields a whole number (no remainder), then the problem is simple.
3
Apply the divisible case
If the share is divisible, the same base number (asl al-mas'alah) used for the first faraid is sufficient for both distributions. No new calculation is needed.
4
Apply the non-divisible case
If not divisible, compute a fresh faraid for the second deceased: determine his base number and his shares. Then multiply the two base numbers together, unless there is a common factor (tawafuq) between the shares of the second deceased and his base number, in which case use only the co-prime part.
5
Check tawafuq
If the shares of the second deceased and the denominator of his faraid share a common divisor greater than 1, divide his denominator by that divisor before multiplying by the first base number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذا لم تقسم التركة حتى مات احد الورثة فان كان مايصيبه من الميت الاول ينقسم على عدد ورثته فقد صحت المسئلتان مما صحت الاولى [۳۲۶۷ (۱۵) وان لم ينقسم صحت فريضة الميت الثانى بالطريقة التي ذكرناها ثم ضربت احدى المسئلتين في الاخرى ان لم يكن بين سهام الميت الثانى وبين ما صحت منه فريضته موافقة
If the estate has not been divided and one of the heirs dies, then if what falls to the first deceased (from the estate) is divisible by the number of his heirs, both problems are valid from what validated the first. [3267 (15)] And if it is not divisible, the faraid of the second deceased is validated by the method we mentioned, then multiply one problem by the other if there is no correspondence (tawafuq) between the shares of the second deceased and that which validated his faraid.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on munasakha (inheritance recalculation) when an heir dies after the first deceased but before the estate is distributed. The first part states the simpler case: if the share that the first deceased (as an heir) received from the original estate is evenly divisible among the number of his own heirs, then no new calculation is needed—the same base number from the first problem works for both distributions. The second part covers the case where it is not divisible: then a fresh faraid calculation must be performed for the second deceased, and then the two base numbers must be combined (by multiplication) to reach a common denominator, unless there is a tawafuq (common factor) between the shares of the second deceased and the denominator of his faraid, in which case only a partial adjustment is needed. The passage provides the general principle; the examples in the surrounding text (with wives, half-sisters, and uncles) illustrate how to apply the method numerically.

Key Terms
مناسخة (munasakhah): A situation in inheritance where one or more heirs die before the estate of the first deceased is distributed, requiring a recalculation of shares to combine the successive deaths.
تقسيم التركة (taqsim al-tarika): The actual distribution of the estate among the heirs according to their prescribed shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Only if there is tawafuq between the shares
D) Only if the first deceased had no heirs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: if the deceased heir's share divides evenly among his heirs, no new calculation; otherwise, compute a new faraid and combine bases, adjusting for tawafuq.

Direct Division in Inheritance: When No Correction is Needed
Faraid case where shares divide evenly among heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when an inheritance problem is correct without further multiplication and calculate the shares of heirs when the division is direct.

Core Idea
When the number of shares equals the number of heirs, the division is direct and no tashih is needed.

In faraid, after a second death, we often need to correct the shares so that each heir receives a whole number. However, sometimes the shares are already divisible by the number of heirs. This lesson examines a simple case where the wife dies leaving exactly five half-brothers and five shares, making the problem correct by itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second deceased and her heirs
The wife dies leaving five paternal half-brothers as sole heirs. No other heirs are present.
Half-brothers inherit as asabah when there is no closer male relative.
2
Determine the estate of the second deceased
The wife's estate consists of 5 shares (from her husband's inheritance).
The text uses 5 as the base number for simplicity.
3
Divide the shares by the number of heirs
5 shares ÷ 5 heirs = 1 share each. Since the division yields an integer for each heir, the problem is correct.
No need for tashih (correction) because the division is direct.
4
Confirm the sihhat of the problem
The original asl al-masalah of 5 is sufficient. No multiplication is required.
This is called 'saheeh min dhatihi' (correct in itself).

Commentary
This passage presents a faraid (inheritance) case and the rule of tashih (correction) when shares are divisible. The text begins by stating two scenarios: either the shares of the first deceased divide evenly among the heirs of the second deceased (al-awwal yanqasimu) or they do not. The example given illustrates the first scenario. A man dies leaving a wife, who later dies leaving five paternal half-brothers (baap shariq bhai). The wife had received 5 shares from her husband's estate (the example simplifies by showing 5 shares as the entire estate). When she dies, her estate consists of those 5 shares, and she leaves exactly 5 half-brothers. Since the number of shares (5) equals the number of heirs (5), each brother receives exactly 1 share. No further multiplication or correction (tashih) is needed because the division is direct (hadd mushtarak). The text then shows a numerical diagram: the deceased's estate is represented as 100 units; the wife gets 25 (1/4), the paternal half-sister gets 50 (1/2), and the remainder 25 goes to the half-brothers. Upon the wife's death, her 25 shares are divided among 5 brothers, each receiving 5. The key legal takeaway is that when the shares of the second deceased are divisible by the number of her heirs without remainder (i.e., they are mutawafiq or exactly equal), the original correction (sihhat) suffices, and no further multiplication is required.

Key Terms
ينقسم (yanqasimu): In this context, it means the shares of the second deceased are divisible by the number of heirs without remainder.
عصبة (asabah): Residuary heirs who inherit the remaining estate after Quranic shares are assigned; here the half-brothers inherit as asabah because no closer male relative exists.
صحت (sihhat): The property of the inheritance problem being correct, meaning shares can be distributed without fractions after applying tashih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a wife dies leaving 5 half-brothers and she has exactly 5 shares from her husband's estate, what is the share of each half-brother?
A) 1/5
B) 1
C) 2
D) 5

Key Takeaway
When the second deceased's shares are exactly divisible by the number of her heirs, the inheritance problem is correct without any correction.

Tashih When Shares Are Not Divisible (Tabayun)
Faraid case requiring tashih due to tabayun between shares and heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the corrected inheritance base when there is tabayun between the shares of the second deceased and the number of her heirs, and distribute the shares accordingly.

Core Idea
When shares and heirs are coprime (tabayun), multiply the entire base by the number of heirs.

This passage explains the second scenario of tashih in inheritance: when the shares of a deceased heir and the number of her own heirs are not divisible (tabayun). It demonstrates the procedure through a concrete example involving a wife, a full sister, and three uncles as original heirs, and then the wife's death leaving five half-brothers. Understanding this process is crucial for correctly distributing estates when multiple deaths occur and shares need to be adjusted.

Guided Path
1
Identify original heirs and shares
For the first deceased, list heirs and their Quranic shares. Here: wife (1/4), full sister (1/2), three paternal uncles (residue). Base = 4.
The original base is the LCM of the share denominators (4).
2
Apply first tashih for indivisible residue
The residue after wife and sister is 1 share, but there are 3 uncles. Since 1 and 3 are coprime, multiply base by 3. New base = 12. Shares: wife=3, sister=6, each uncle=1.
Tabayun between residue (1) and number of uncles (3).
3
Identify second death and her heirs
The wife (second deceased) dies leaving 5 half-brothers. Her share from the first estate is 3.
We now focus on the wife's estate within the first estate's corrected base.
4
Determine tabayun and multiply base
Wife's shares (3) and number of her heirs (5) are coprime (gcd=1). Multiply current base (12) by 5. New base = 60.
If they had a common factor, we would multiply by the factor (muwafaqah).
5
Multiply all shares and distribute wife's share
Multiply each share: wife's 3→15, sister's 6→30, each uncle's 1→3. Then divide wife's 15 among her 5 half-brothers: each gets 3. Final base=60, each half-brother=3, sister=30, each uncle=3.
All shares are now whole numbers.

Commentary
This passage presents the second scenario of tashih in inheritance: when the shares of the second deceased and the number of her heirs are not divisible (tabayun, meaning they are coprime). The text explains that if there is no concord (muwafaqah), meaning the shares and the heirs have no common factor other than 1, then the number of the second heir (the number of heirs of the second deceased) is multiplied by the original problem's base (asl al-masalah). The resulting product becomes the new corrected base, and the shares are multiplied accordingly. An example is given: The first deceased leaves a wife (1/4), a full sister (1/2), and three paternal uncles (asabah). The original base is 4 (from the fractions 1/4 and 1/2, LCM=4). The shares: wife gets 1, sister gets 2, uncles get 1 (residue). Since there are three uncles and only 1 share to divide among them, tashih is first applied by multiplying the base by 3 (the number of uncles), giving 12. After that, the wife has 3 shares, sister 6, each uncle 1. Now, the second deceased (the wife) dies leaving her share of 3 to her heirs: 5 half-brothers (from the husband? Actually the text says '5 brothers' at the end, not clear from the first list). There is tabayun between 3 (her shares) and 5 (number of her heirs); they have no common factor. So the corrected base (12) is multiplied by 5, giving 60. The shares are then multiplied: wife's 3 becomes 15, sister's 6 becomes 30, uncles' 1 each becomes 3 each (total 3×3=9? Actually 3 uncles each get 3, total 9, plus wife's 15, sister's 30 = 54? Wait, the text shows 15+3=5? That seems off. Actually the calculation at the end shows: 3×5=15 for wife, 6×5=30 for sister, 3×5=15 for uncles (total 60). But the uncles were 3, each gets 1 originally, so after multiplication each gets 3, total 9? But the text says 3×5=15 for uncles collectively? There is inconsistency. The example may intend that after the wife's death, her shares go to her half-brothers, not the uncles. The final distribution: wife's 15 shares are divided among 5 half-brothers, each getting 3. The text says 'هر ایک کو 3 ملے گا' (each will get 3). So the wife's heirs are the 5 half-brothers, and they each get 3 from her 15 shares. The sister's and uncles' shares remain with them. So the final corrected base is 60: wife's share 15 goes to brothers, sister keeps 30, uncles keep 15 collectively (each 5? Actually 15 among 3 uncles is 5 each, but the text says each uncle gets 3? There is confusion. The example may be poorly transmitted. The key legal principle is clear: when there is tabayun between the second deceased's shares and the number of her heirs, multiply the first problem's corrected base by the number of heirs of the second deceased, then multiply all shares accordingly, and then divide the second deceased's new shares among her heirs.

Key Terms
تبائن (tabāyun): Discrepancy; when two numbers (here the shares of the second deceased and the number of her heirs) have no common factor greater than 1.
موافقت (muwāfaqah): Concord; when two numbers share a common factor greater than 1. In such cases, only the common factor is used for multiplication, not the entire number.
تصیح (taṣḥīḥ): The process of adjusting the base number (asl al-masalah) so that all shares become whole numbers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When the second deceased's shares and her number of heirs are coprime (tabayun), the entire base is multiplied by the number of heirs, and all shares are scaled accordingly.

Tabāyun between Shares and Number of Heirs
Explains method for tabāyun when distributing shares to multiple heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tabāyun rule to correct inheritance distribution when the number of heirs and the share are mutually non-divisible.

Core Idea
When heirs cannot divide a share evenly, multiply the base by the number of those heirs.

In Hanafi inheritance, after determining shares, you may face a situation where a deceased heir's share cannot be divided equally among his or her own heirs. This lesson walks through a case where a wife dies after correction, leaving five brothers, and her share (3 units) is in tabāyun with the number of brothers (5). You will learn the classical method to resolve this by multiplying the base.

Guided Path
1
Identify original shares and base
Start with the first deceased: wife (1/4), full sister (1/2), three paternal uncles (residue). Base = 4. Shares: wife=1, sister=2, uncles=1 (to be divided among three).
The base 4 is the LCM of denominators 4 and 2.
2
Perform initial tashīh for uncles
The uncles' share (1) cannot be divided among 3 without fractions, so multiply base 4 by 3 → 12. New shares: wife=3, sister=6, uncles=3 (each 1).
Tashīh corrects fractional shares by multiplying base by the number of heirs in a group that cannot divide their share.
3
Recognize second death and tabāyun
Before distribution, the wife dies leaving 5 brothers. Her share is now 3 units. Compare 3 and 5: they share no common divisor → tabāyun.
Tabāyun means the two numbers are co-prime.
4
Apply tabāyun rule: multiply base by number of second heirs
Multiply the corrected base 12 by 5 (the number of brothers) → 60. This becomes the new common base.
The rule: if between the share of the second deceased and the number of her heirs there is tabāyun, multiply the base by that number.
5
Distribute the new shares
Multiply each heir's share from the first correction by 5: wife=3×5=15, sister=6×5=30, uncles=3×5=15. The wife's 15 goes to her 5 brothers: each gets 3.
Final distribution: sister gets 30, each uncle gets 1 (since 15/3=5? Wait, careful: the uncles' 15 divided among 3 gives each 5? کلکولیٹر کے حساب میں تبائن، تماثل، تداخل اور توافق کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ کسر کے ذریعہ سے سب پر تقسیم کر دیا جائے گا۔
Chapter: Calculation of Inheritances. If between the shares of the second deceased and what the estate was corrected from there is no correspondence, but rather mutual opposition, then multiply the number of second heirs by the original base, then divide the resulting shares by the number of second heirs, and the problem will be correct. Example: The deceased left wife, full sister, and three uncles. Therefore the base goes from four, and the correction from twelve. The problem is set up like this: Deceased 4, correction 12, correction 60. Wife, one full sister, 3×4=12x5=60, 3 uncles, 3,2,1,3,6,3,15,30,15. Wife died / deceased. After correction, 3 uncles got; after correction sister got; after correction wife got; after distribution each uncle got; after distribution one sister got; after distribution one wife got; after distribution each brother got. 5x12=60, 5 brothers, 3,15, each gets 3. Hand has 3. 3x5=15, 6x5=30, 3x5=15, 60, 15+3=5, 30+1=30, 15÷1=15, 15÷5=3. Method of correction: In this problem there were three uncles and they got one each, so multiply the original base four by three, gives correction from twelve. Then the wife had one, so multiply it by three, yielding three which becomes wife's share. The sister had two, multiply by three, she gets six. The uncle had one, now multiply by three, gives three, so each uncle gets one. Then before distribution the wife died, leaving five brothers. Now the wife has only three shares and there are five brothers, which cannot be divided. And between three and five there is opposition. So multiply the original base twelve by five, becomes sixty. Now the heirs of both deceased get shares from sixty. And those who already received, their shares are multiplied by five. Thus the wife had three from twelve, multiply by five gives fifteen; so the wife now has fifteen shares. And the five brothers take by 'asaba the entire property, each brother gets three each. Look carefully at the calculation above. This example is of opposition between the heirs of both deceased. The calculator's calculation will be like this: Deceased 100, wife, one full sister, 3 uncles, 25, wife died/deceased, 50, 25 in hand, 5 brothers, 25, 25, each gets 5, 25+5=5. In the calculator's calculation, opposition, similarity, intercalation, and correspondence are not considered; it is distributed by fraction.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents a faraid case illustrating the correction method when there is tabāyun (mutual non-divisibility) between the shares of a deceased heir and the number of his/her heirs. The author first states the general rule: if there is no correspondence (muwāfaqah) but rather opposition (tabāyun) between the shares of the second deceased and the number from which the estate was corrected, then multiply the number of the second heir(s) by the original base (asl al-mas'alah). Then the resulting shares are divided among those heirs. The example: a man dies leaving wife, full sister, and three paternal uncles. The original base is 4 (wife gets 1/4 = 1 share, sister gets 1/2 = 2 shares, uncles get the residue = 1 share). But since the uncles are three and cannot divide 1 share, we multiply by 3 to get base 12, then tashīh (correction) results in wife 3, sister 6, uncles 3 (each 1). The wife dies before distribution, leaving five brothers. Her shares are now 3, and the number of brothers is 5, which are in tabāyun (no common factor other than 1). So we multiply the original base 12 by 5, obtaining new base 60. Now the wife's share becomes 3×5=15, and the brothers as 'asabah divide the 15 each getting 3. The text also shows a calculator method that ignores these relations and simply divides fractions, arriving at 5 shares per brother. The passage emphasizes the classical Hanafi method for correcting fractional shares.

Key Terms
تبائن (tabāyun): Mutual opposition between two numbers meaning they have no common divisor other than 1.
تصحیح (tashīh): Correction of the base number to make shares integer when heirs cannot divide a share.
اصل مسئلہ (asl al-mas'alah): The original base number from which shares are calculated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first step when the number of heirs cannot divide the share (tabāyun) in Hanafi inheritance?
A) Divide the share among heirs arbitrarily.
B) Multiply the original base by the number of those heirs.
C) Reduce the share to zero.
D) Add the share to the spouse's portion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance, when a deceased heir's share and the number of his or her heirs are mutually non-divisible (tabāyun), multiply the original base by the number of those heirs.

Tawāfuq in Faraid: Correcting Inheritance Shares
Faraid correction method with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of tawāfuq to correct inheritance when the share of a group of heirs and their number share a common divisor.

Core Idea
When shares and heirs are in tawāfuq, multiply by the wafq — the number after cancelling the common divisor.

This lesson unpacks a key correction technique in faraid: tawāfuq. Classical texts present a rule and an example involving a wife, sister, and five uncles. By walking through the method, you will learn how to harmonise the original estate base with the number of heirs, ensuring each receives an integer share. The example reveals a subtle point about defining tawāfuq that is often misunderstood.

Guided Path
1
List heirs and original shares
Identify each heir and their share from the original base. In the example: wife 1/4 (1 share), sister 1/2 (2 shares), five uncles residue (1 share).
The base is 4.
2
Focus on the group with multiple heirs
The uncles are multiple and cannot divide the 1 share. Note the number of uncles (5) and their share in base units (1).
The key is the relationship between these two numbers.
3
Determine tawāfuq and compute wafq
Check if the share (1) and number (5) have a common divisor. The GCD is 1, which divides both. In Hanafi terms, this is tawāfuq. The wafq is the number divided by the GCD: 5/1=5.
Some call this tabāyun, but the Hanafi method still multiplies by the wafq, which here is the full number.
4
Multiply original base by wafq
Multiply the original base (4) by the wafq (5) to get the new base 20.
New base is the corrected denominator for both problems.
5
Distribute new shares
Assign new shares: wife 5, sister 10, uncles 5 (each uncle gets 1). Check that all are integers.
This corrects the first estate and sets up the second problem correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۲۲۸ (۶) فان كانت سهامهم موافقة فاضرب وفق المسئلة الثانية في الاولى فما اجتمع صحت منه المسئلتان
یہ وفق کی مثال ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو وارث مرا ہے اس کو جو حصہ ملا اس میں اور جس سے مسئلہ چلے گا اس میں تو افق کی نسبت ہے تو توافق سے پہلے میت کے اصل میں ضرب دیں۔ جو حاصل ضرب ہوگا اس سے دونوں مسئلوں کی تصحیح ہو جائے گی۔ مثلا میت نے ایک بیوی، ایک باپ شریک بہن اور پانچ چا چھوڑے۔ اس لئے مسئلہ چار سے چلے گا۔ لیکن پانچ چا کو ایک حصہ ملے گا جو ان پر تقسیم نہیں ہو سکے گا۔ اس لئے پانچ کو چار اصل مسئلہ سے ضرب دیا جائے گا تو بیس ہوگا۔ اور اسی میں سے میت اول کی تصیح ہوگی۔
If their shares correspond, then multiply the factor of the second base (after canceling common divisor) into the first base; from that product both problems will be corrected. This is an example of correspondence. Meaning: there is a correspondence between the share the deceased heir received and the number from which the problem will run, so multiply the original base by the factor (wafq) of the deceased's (heir's) portion. The product will correct both problems. For example, a deceased left a wife, a full sister by father, and five uncles. So the base goes from four. But the five uncles get one share which cannot be divided among them. So multiply five by four, gives twenty. And from that the first deceased's correction is made.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents a faraid case illustrating the correction method when there is tawāfuq (mutual divisibility) between the shares of a deceased heir and the number of his/her heirs. The rule: if the shares and the number of heirs have a common divisor (i.e., they are proportional), then multiply the original base by the fraction of the number of heirs after canceling the common divisor (i.e., the wafq). The example: a man dies leaving wife, a sister (sharīk bāp? possibly 'paternal sister' or 'half-sister'), and five uncles. The original base is 4 (wife gets 1/4 = 1 share, sister? likely gets 1/2 = 2 shares, uncles get residue = 1 share). But the uncles are five and cannot divide 1 share. However, 5 and 1 are in tawāfuq? Actually 1 divides any number, so the wafq is 1? The text says multiply 5 by 4 to get 20. That would be if we multiply the base by the number of heirs directly, not by the wafq. But the passage mentions 'وافق' and then says to multiply the 'wafq' (the factor after reduction) of the second base into the first. The example multiplies 5 by 4, so perhaps the wafq is 5 because 1 and 5 are co-prime? Actually 1 and 5 have no common divisor, so that is tabāyun, not tawāfuq. There seems to be confusion; the example might be misstated, or the term 'موافقة' is used loosely to include both? Since the passage explicitly calls it a case of correspondence, we follow the text and present the method as described, noting the internal inconsistency. The key point: when there is a common factor, cancel it; otherwise multiply by the full number.

Key Terms
موافقة (muwāfaqah/tawāfuq): Correspondence between two numbers meaning they share a common divisor, allowing cancellation.
وفق (wafq): The number after canceling the common divisor; the factor to multiply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 4
B) 5
C) 20
D) 16

Key Takeaway
When shares and heirs are in tawāfuq, multiply the original base by the wafq (number of heirs divided by their GCD) to obtain a corrected base that yields integer shares.

Method of Tabayn in Correcting Shares When an Heir Dies Before Distribution
Explains tabayn method with example of wife dying leaving 5 brothers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tabayn method to correct inheritance shares when the number of shares held by the deceased heir and the number of new heirs are mutually prime.

Core Idea
When shares and heirs are coprime, multiply the entire number of heirs into the original base.

The great Hanafi jurists developed systematic methods to correct inheritance fractions without resorting to continuous division. One such method is tabayn, used when the number of shares held by the deceased heir and the number of their own heirs are mutually prime. In this lesson, you will learn to identify tabayn and apply the correct multiplication to adjust the original base.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deceased heir's share from the first estate
Determine how many units of the original base the deceased heir received. In the example, the wife had 3 out of 12.
2
Identify the number of new heirs of that deceased heir
Count the heirs of the second deceased. Here, the wife left 5 brothers.
3
Check if the share and the number are mutually prime (tabayn)
Find the GCD. If GCD = 1, they are tabayn. 3 and 5 have GCD 1.
4
Multiply the entire number of new heirs into the original base
Multiply 5 (brothers) by 12 (original base) to get a new universal base 60.
This avoids fractions.
5
Adjust all original shares by multiplying each by that same number
Multiply the wife's share (3) by 5 to get 15, and each brother gets 3 (15/5).
All heirs from the first estate have their shares multiplied by 5.

(3228 (6)) 'If their shares are compatible, multiply the agreed portion (wafq) of the second problem into the first, and that product corrects both problems.' And before this distribution, the wife died, leaving five brothers. So the wife had only three shares, and the brothers are five, so distribution cannot be done. And three and five are mutually prime. So multiply five into the original base twelve, resulting in sixty. Thus the heirs of both deceased receive shares from sixty. In the calculator calculation, tabayn, tamathul, tadakhul, and tawafuq are not considered; distribution is done by fraction. (3268) (16) 'And if their shares are compatible, then multiply the second problem's agreed portion into the first problem; then the product corrects both problems.' This is an example of wafq. Means: the deceased's heir who died had a share that is compatible with the base of his problem. So multiply by the wafq of the first deceased's base. That product corrects both problems. For example: the deceased left a wife, a paternal half-sister, and five paternal uncles. So the problem proceeds from four. But the five uncles get one share which is not divisible among them, so multiply five by the original base four to get twenty. And from that the first deceased is corrected. The problem becomes: deceased 4, corrected 20, final correction 120. Wife, half-sister, 5 uncles: ... After correction, the 5 uncles get... After correction, the sister gets... After correction, the wife gets... Re-correction: the 5 uncles get..., the sister gets..., the wife gets... 5x1=5, 5x2=10, 5x1=5, 6x5=30, 6x10=60, 6x5=30. Method of tashih. Before distribution, the half-sister died leaving a husband, mother, one daughter, and a paternal uncle. So the problem proceeds from twelve. But the difficulty is that the sister had ten shares, and the problem proceeds from twelve which is larger. However, ten and twelve have a common factor: both are divisible by two. So half of twelve (six) is multiplied into the first base problem, resulting in one hundred twenty. And the sister's ten is multiplied by six to get sixty. Now sixty is distributed among the sister's heirs. The problem becomes: deceased 12, correction 60; husband, mother, one daughter, paternal uncle: 3,2,6,1 respectively; then multiplied: 30,10,15,5. Since there was tawafuq by two, half of ten is five. Multiply all heirs' shares by five to get shares. As seen: husband's 3 shares times 5 = 15, mother's 2 times 5 = 10, uncle's 1 times 5 = 5, total 60, which the half-sister received from the first deceased.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi faraid commentary, explaining how to correct inheritance shares when an heir dies before the final distribution. The text presents two scenarios: tabayn (mutual prime) and muwafaqa (compatible numbers). In the tabayn example, the wife dies after receiving 3 out of 12 shares, leaving 5 brothers. Since 3 and 5 are coprime, we multiply the entire second base (5) into the first base (12) to get a common base of 60. Then we adjust the wife's share to 15 and each brother gets 3. In the muwafaqa example, the first deceased leaves a wife, a paternal half-sister, and 5 paternal uncles. The base is 4, but the uncles' 1 share is indivisible, so first we multiply 5 into 4 to get 20. Then the half-sister dies, leaving a husband, mother, daughter, and paternal uncle. Her share from the first estate is 10, but the second estate's base is 12. Since 10 and 12 share a common factor of 2, we take the wafq (6) and multiply it into the first corrected base (20) to get 120. Then we adjust all shares accordingly. The commentary emphasizes the importance of identifying tabayn, tamathul, tadakhul, and tawafuq between the numbers to apply the correct method.

Key Terms
تباین (tabayn): Two numbers that have no common factor other than 1 (mutually prime).
تصحیح (tashih): The process of adjusting the base number to avoid fractional shares. Here it means multiplying the base by a factor to make shares whole numbers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the tabayn example, what was the original base of the first estate?
A) 3
B) 5
C) 12
D) 60

Key Takeaway
When shares and new heirs are coprime, multiply the entire number of heirs into the original base to correct all shares.

Faraid — Inheritance

Method of Muwafaqa in Correcting Shares When an Heir Dies Before Distribution
Explains muwafaqa method with example of half-sister dying leaving husband, mother, daughter, and uncle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the muwafaqa method to correct inheritance shares when the share of the deceased heir and the new base are compatible, using the wafq factor.

Core Idea
When shares and base are compatible, multiply only the wafq (base divided by GCD) into the original corrected base.

When the share of the deceased heir and the base of the new problem share a common divisor, we do not multiply the entire base but only the agreed portion (wafq). This lesson shows how to identify muwafaqa and apply the correct factor, using the example of a half-sister who dies leaving a husband, mother, daughter, and uncle.

Guided Path
1
Determine the deceased heir's share from the first corrected estate
After any initial tashih, find the share of the heir who dies. Here the half-sister had 10 out of a corrected base of 20.
2
Determine the base of the new problem (for the second deceased)
Calculate the base from the second deceased's heirs. Here the second base is 12 (husband 3, mother 2, daughter 6, uncle 1).
3
Find the GCD of the share and the new base; compute wafq as new base ÷ GCD
10 and 12 have GCD 2. Wafq = 12 ÷ 2 = 6.
4
Multiply the wafq into the first corrected base
Multiply 6 into the first corrected base (20) to get a global base 120.
5
Adjust the second estate by its own tashih factor
The share of the deceased heir becomes 10 × wafq = 60. Then divide 60 by the second base (12) to get factor 5. Multiply each second heir's share by 5 to get their shares from 120.
Final shares: husband 15, mother 10, daughter 30, uncle 5.

5 5x2 = 10 5x1 = 5 6x5 = 30 6x10 = 60 6x5 = 30 تفصیح کا طریقہ تصحیح کا طریقہ ابھی تقسیم ہوئی بھی نہیں تھی کہ باپ شریک بہن مرگئی اور شوہر، ماں، ایک بیٹی اور چا چھوڑا ۔ اس لئے مسئلہ بارہ سے چلے گا۔ لیکن مشکل یہ ہے کہ بہن کے ہاتھ میں دس حصے ہیں اور مسئلہ بارہ سے چل رہا ہے جو زیادہ ہے۔ البتہ دس اور بارہ میں تو افق ہے۔ دونوں دو سے فنا ہو جاتے ہیں۔ اس لئے بارہ کا آدھا چھ سے میت اول کے اصل مسئلہ میں میں ضرب دیا تو حاصل ضرب ایک سو میں ہو جائے گا۔ اور بہن کے ہاتھ میں دس کو چھ سے ضرب دیں گے تو ساتھ ہو جائے گا۔ اب ساتھ بہن کے وارثوں پر تقسیم ہوگا ۔ مسئلہ اس طرح بنے گا۔ 60=10x6 ہاتھ میں ہے میت 12 تصحیح 60 شوہر ماں ایک بیٹی 1 5 6 2 3 30 10 15 اس مسئلے میں چونکہ دو سے تو افق تھا اس لئے دس کا آدھا پانچ ہو گا۔ اور تمام وارثوں کے حصوں کو پانچ سے ضرب دینے سے سب وارثوں کے حصے نکل جائیں گے۔ جیسا کہ اوپر حساب میں دیکھ رہے ہیں کہ شوہر کے تین حصوں کو پانچ سے ضرب دیا تو تصحیح پندرہ سے ہوا۔ اور ماں کے دو حصوں کو پانچ سے ضرب دیا تو دس ہو گیا۔ اور چچا کے ایک حصے کو پانچ سے ضرب دیا تو پانچ ہو گیا۔ اور مجموعہ ساٹھ حصے ہو گئے جو باپ شریک بہن کے ہاتھ میں پہلی میت سے ملے تھے۔
In the chapter on calculation of inheritance. (3228 (6)) 'If their shares are compatible, multiply the agreed portion (wafq) of the second problem into the first, and that product corrects both problems.' And before this distribution, the wife died, leaving five brothers. So the wife had only three shares, and the brothers are five, so distribution cannot be done. And three and five are mutually prime. So multiply five into the original base twelve, resulting in sixty. Thus the heirs of both deceased receive shares from sixty. And those who already received something, we multiply their shares by five. So the wife initially had three from twelve; multiply by five gives fifteen. So the wife now has fifteen shares. And the five brothers take as asaba, each gets three. Examine the above calculation; this example is of tabayn between the heirs of the two deceased. The calculator account will be like this: ... In the calculator calculation, tabayn, tamathul, tadakhul, and tawafuq are not considered; distribution is done by fraction. (3268) (16) 'And if their shares are compatible, then multiply the second problem's agreed portion into the first problem; then the product corrects both problems.' This is an example of wafq. Means: the deceased's heir who died had a share that is compatible with the base of his problem. So multiply by the wafq of the first deceased's base. That product corrects both problems. For example: the deceased left a wife, a paternal half-sister, and five paternal uncles. So the problem proceeds from four. But the five uncles get one share which is not divisible among them, so multiply five by the original base four to get twenty. And from that the first deceased is corrected. The problem becomes: deceased 4, corrected 20, final correction 120. Wife, half-sister, 5 uncles: ... After correction, the 5 uncles get... After correction, the sister gets... After correction, the wife gets... Re-correction: the 5 uncles get..., the sister gets..., the wife gets... 5x1=5, 5x2=10, 5x1=5, 6x5=30, 6x10=60, 6x5=30. Method of tashih. Before distribution, the half-sister died leaving a husband, mother, one daughter, and a paternal uncle. So the problem proceeds from twelve. But the difficulty is that the sister had ten shares, and the problem proceeds from twelve which is larger. However, ten and twelve have a common factor: both are divisible by two. So half of twelve (six) is multiplied into the first base problem, resulting in one hundred twenty. And the sister's ten is multiplied by six to get sixty. Now sixty is distributed among the sister's heirs. The problem becomes: deceased 12, correction 60; husband, mother, one daughter, paternal uncle: 3,2,6,1 respectively; then multiplied: 30,10,15,5. Since there was tawafuq by two, half of ten is five. Multiply all heirs' shares by five to get shares. As seen: husband's 3 shares times 5 = 15, mother's 2 times 5 = 10, uncle's 1 times 5 = 5, total 60, which the half-sister received from the first deceased.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi faraid commentary, explaining how to correct inheritance shares when an heir dies before the final distribution. The text presents two scenarios: tabayn (mutual prime) and muwafaqa (compatible numbers). In the tabayn example, the wife dies after receiving 3 out of 12 shares, leaving 5 brothers. Since 3 and 5 are coprime, we multiply the entire second base (5) into the first base (12) to get a common base of 60. Then we adjust the wife's share to 15 and each brother gets 3. In the muwafaqa example, the first deceased leaves a wife, a paternal half-sister, and 5 paternal uncles. The base is 4, but the uncles' 1 share is indivisible, so first we multiply 5 into 4 to get 20. Then the half-sister dies, leaving a husband, mother, daughter, and paternal uncle. Her share from the first estate is 10, but the second estate's base is 12. Since 10 and 12 share a common factor of 2, we take the wafq (6) and multiply it into the first corrected base (20) to get 120. Then we adjust all shares accordingly. The commentary emphasizes the importance of identifying tabayn, tamathul, tadakhul, and tawafuq between the numbers to apply the correct method.

Key Terms
موافقة (muwafaqa): Two numbers that share a common factor greater than 1 (not mutually prime).
وفق (wafq): A common factor between two numbers; the part of the larger number after dividing by the common factor. In the text, wafq of 12 regarding 10 is 6 (since GCD=2, 12/2=6).
تصحیح (tashih): Correction process; here used for both initial (by 5) and final (by 5) adjustments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the muwafaqa example, what is the GCD of the half-sister's share (10) and the second base (12)?
A) 1
B) 2
C) 5
D) 6

Key Takeaway
When shares and base are compatible, multiply only the wafq (base ÷ GCD) into the first corrected base to achieve the global tashih.

Faraid — Inheritance

Munasakhah Calculation: Redistribution After an Heir's Death
Demonstrates inheritance redistribution when an heir dies before distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the redistribution of an estate when an heir dies before distribution, using both the traditional tas-hih method and the calculator method.

Core Idea
When an heir dies before distribution, their share becomes a new estate for their own heirs.

Inheritance distribution is straightforward when all heirs survive until the estate is divided. But what happens when an heir dies before receiving their share? This situation, called munasakhah, requires us to treat the deceased heir's share as a new estate to be distributed among their own heirs. In this lesson, we work through a multi-step example involving three deaths, using the classical tas-hih method and a modern calculator approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first deceased and their heirs
List all heirs and assign their Quranic shares. For a wife, consanguine sister, and paternal uncle, the wife gets 1/4, sister 1/2, uncle 1/4 as asabah. Find a common base (4) and scale to avoid fractions (20).
Scaling to 20 gives integer shares: wife 5, sister 10, uncle 5.
2
Handle the second death of the sister
The sister dies before distribution, leaving her own heirs: husband, mother, daughter, and the uncle (again). Determine their shares: husband 1/4, mother 1/6, daughter 1/2, uncle 1/12. Base = 12.
The sister's estate is her share from the first death: 10 (scaled to 20) or 60 after further scaling.
3
Find tawafuq between the two estates
Compare the sister's share (10 from base 20) with the second death's base (12). Both are divisible by 2 (tawafuq). Halve each: 5 and 6. Use these to scale both estates to a common number.
First estate base (20) × 6 = 120. Sister's share (10) × 6 = 60. Second estate base (12) × 5 = 60.
4
Distribute the sister's estate among her heirs
The sister's scaled share (60) is divided among her heirs according to their scaled shares from second death: husband 15, mother 10, daughter 30, uncle 5. Add uncle's share from first estate (30) to get his total (35).
The uncle inherits from both deaths: 30 from first + 5 from second.
5
Extend to a third death (optional)
If the daughter now dies, treat her share (30) as a new estate. Her heirs: husband, son, daughter. Shares: husband 1/4, remainder to son and daughter in 2:1 ratio. Use the calculator method with base 100 for simplicity.
The calculator method scales each death to 100 and then multiplies by the actual share proportionally.

اس دوران باپ شریک بہن زاہدہ مرتی اور اس کے ہاتھ میں 50 تھا۔ اور وارثین میں شوہر ، ماں، ایک بیٹی اور چچا چھوڑا۔ تو پہلے ان وارثین کو 100 سے ان کے حصوں کے مطابق تقسیم کریں۔ اس طرح شوہر کو چوتھائی 25 ملے گا، ماں کو چھٹا 16.66 ملے گا، بیٹی کو آدھا 50 ملے گا اور جو 8.34 بچے وہ چچا کو بطور عصبہ ملے گا۔

50 ÷ 100 = 0.5 یہ ایک حصہ ہوا۔

The problem will be set up as follows. The deceased 4 corrected to 20 / correction to 120. Wife, consanguine sister, 5 uncle. 1,2,1. 5,10,5. 30,60,30. Chapter of Calculation of Inheritances. After correction, the uncle got 5. After correction, the sister got 10. After correction, the wife got 5. After second correction, the uncle got 30. After second correction, the sister got 60. After second correction, the wife got 30. 5x1=5, 5x2=10, 5x1=5, 6x5=30, 6x10=60, 6x5=30. But the distribution had not yet occurred when the consanguine sister died, leaving a husband, a mother, one daughter, and an uncle. So the problem will run from twelve. But the difficulty is that the sister had ten shares in her hands and the problem runs from twelve, which is larger. However, ten and twelve have tawafuq (common factor). Both are divisible by two. Therefore, half of twelve, which is six, multiplied by the original problem of the first deceased yields one hundred twenty. And the sister's ten multiplied by six yields sixty. Now sixty will be distributed among the sister's heirs. The problem will be set up as follows. She has 60 = 10x6. Deceased 12, correction 60. Husband, Mother, One daughter. 1,5,6,2,3,30,10,15. In this problem, since there was tawafuq with two, half of ten is five. Multiplying all heirs' shares by five gives all heirs' shares. As seen in the calculation above, multiplying the husband's three shares by five gives fifteen corrected. Multiplying the mother's two shares by five gives ten. Multiplying the uncle's one share by five gives five. And the total becomes sixty shares, which the consanguine sister received from the first deceased. The calculator's calculation will be as follows. 100. Wife, consanguine sister, 5 uncle. 25,50,25. The sister died / deceased 100. Husband, mother, one daughter, uncle. 25,16.66,50,8.34. Chapter of Calculation of Inheritances. The new method of munasakhah. The method to do munasakhah with a calculator is: first give each heir of the first deceased their share from 100. In the above example, we made the problem from 100 and gave the wife 25, the sister 50, and the uncle 25. Meanwhile, the consanguine sister Zahidah died and she had 50 in her hands. She left a husband, mother, one daughter, and an uncle. First, distribute among those heirs from 100 according to their shares. Thus the husband gets one-fourth 25, the mother gets one-sixth 16.66, the daughter gets half 50, and the remainder 8.34 goes to the uncle as asabah. Now she had 50 and we have distributed from 100. So the method is to divide 50 by 100, and what comes out is one share. Then multiply that by all the heirs' shares, so each heir gets their portion from the amount she had. The division will be as follows: 50 ÷ 100 = 0.5. This is one share. Then: multiply the husband's share 25.00 by that gives 12.5 which goes to the husband. Multiply the mother's share 16.66 by that gives 8.33 which goes to the mother. Multiply the daughter's share 50.00 by that gives 25.00 which goes to the daughter. Multiply the uncle's share 8.34 by that gives 4.17 which goes to the uncle. The total is 50. Now suppose the daughter Rashidah died, leaving a husband, one son, and one daughter Zainab. Then make the problem from 100, give the husband one-fourth 25. And the remaining 75 is given to the son and daughter with the male having twice the share of the female, i.e., the son gets double 50 and the daughter gets one portion 25. But daughter Rashidah only received 25 from the inheritance, so divide 25 by 100, and 0.25 will come out as one share.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a worked example of munasakhah (inheritance redistribution when an heir dies before distribution). The author demonstrates two methods: the traditional tas-hih method and a calculator method. 

First, the initial deceased leaves a wife, a consanguine sister, and an uncle. Their shares are: wife 1/4, sister 1/2, uncle 1/4 as asabah. The base is 4, but the author scales it to 20 for convenience (giving shares 5, 10, 5) and then to 120 (30, 60, 30) to prepare for combining with the second death.

Before the first estate is distributed, the sister dies. She leaves a husband, mother, one daughter, and the same uncle. The sister's estate is 60 (her share from the first death). The second death's base is 12 (husband 1/4=3, mother 1/6=2, daughter 1/2=6, uncle 1/12=1). There is tawafuq (common factor 2) between 10 (the sister's original share) and 12 (the second death's base). So half of 10 is 5 and half of 12 is 6. The author multiplies the first estate's base (20) by 6 to get 120, and the sister's share (10) by 6 to get 60. Then the second death's shares (based on 12) are scaled by 5 (half of 10) to get a new base of 60. Thus the sister's 60 is distributed to her heirs: husband 15, mother 10, daughter 30, uncle 5.

Finally, the author introduces the calculator method, which uses 100 as an arbitrary base for each death and then scales proportionally. A third death is also set up: the daughter dies, leaving a husband, son, and daughter. The husband gets 1/4, and the children split the remainder as asabah with the son receiving double the daughter's share.

Throughout, the key principle is finding the common factor (tawafuq) between the heir's share and the new base, then scaling both estates to a common denominator for fair redistribution.

Key Terms
مناسخہ (munāsakhah): The situation where an heir dies before the estate of the first deceased is distributed, requiring the share of the deceased heir to be redistributed among his/her own heirs.
تصحیح (taṣḥīḥ): The process of adjusting the base number of shares to eliminate fractions, often by multiplying the base and shares by a common factor.
توافق (tawāfuq): Two numbers having a common factor greater than 1, used here to find a common multiplier for combining two estates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second death (the sister), what is the share of her daughter before any scaling?
A) 1/4
B) 1/2
C) 1/6
D) 1/12

Key Takeaway
In munasakhah, the share of the deceased heir is treated as a new estate for their own heirs, requiring proportional scaling between the two estates.

Calculator Method for Munasakha Inheritance
Explains calculator-based munasakha with examples and rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the calculator method with base 100 to distribute shares in successive inheritance (munasakha) and explain the two general rules for adjusting shares.

Core Idea
Use base 100 and a conversion factor to simplify successive inheritance distribution.

This lesson presents a practical calculator method for munasakha, where heirs die before receiving their inheritance. By setting a convenient base of 100, you can systematically compute each heir's share across multiple deaths. The method is illustrated through two detailed examples and summarized in two general rules (rules 17 and 18) that handle adjustments when fractions arise.

Guided Path
1
Set up the first estate with base 100
Assume the total estate is 100 (a convenient base). Assign each heir their hypothetical share from this 100 according to their prescribed portions (Quranic shares or residuary). This gives a clear proportional framework.
The actual estate value is arbitrary; 100 is used to avoid fractions.
2
Identify the second deceased and their amount in hand
When an heir from the first estate dies, they leave behind their own heirs. The amount they received from the first estate (their 'in hand' amount) becomes the estate for the second death. Note this amount as the new total to distribute.
In the example, the father's sister had 50 in hand.
3
Calculate the conversion factor
Divide the amount in hand by 100 to get the conversion factor. For example, 50 ÷ 100 = 0.5. This factor scales the hypothetical shares of the second deceased's heirs to match the actual available amount.
The factor is always a decimal between 0 and 1.
4
Distribute to the second set of heirs
First, assign hypothetical shares from 100 for the second deceased's heirs (using the same base method). Then multiply each share by the conversion factor to obtain the actual inheritance. Verify that the sum equals the amount in hand.
In the example: husband: 25 × 0.5 = 12.5, mother: 16.66 × 0.5 = 8.33, daughter: 50 × 0.5 = 25, uncle: 8.34 × 0.5 = 4.17.
5
Apply general rules for adjustments (rules 17 and 18)
When fractional heirs or repeated deaths occur, use rule 17: multiply shares from the first problem by the number (or its wafq) that corrected the second problem. Rule 18: multiply second problem shares by the wafq of the estate of the second deceased. These ensure whole-number shares.
Wafq refers to a common factor that makes shares divisible without fractions.
